<h3>Get Free Account</h3><div class="join-btn">Join Now</div></a></div></div></div><div class="col-md-9 blog-main"><div class="single-post-content"><p>IBM X-Force recently released an excellent <a href="https://securityintelligence.com/new-banking-trojan-icedid-discovered-by-ibm-x-force-research/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow">report </a> on a new banking trojan named IcedID that is being distributed using computers already infected with Emotet. We took the MD5 of one of the droppers from the IBM report and extracted the payload.  After extracting the payload from one of the droppers listed in the report, using <a href="https://intezer.com//intezer-analyze/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Intezer Analyze</a>&#x2122;, we have found code reuse from another malware named Pony, written about in a <a href="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/threat-insight/post/hancitor-ruckguv-reappear" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow">report</a> by Proofpoint.</p>
<blockquote class="twitter-tweet" data-width="550" data-dnt="true">
<p lang="en" dir="ltr"><a href="https://twitter.com/hashtag/IcedID?src=hash&amp;ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw">#IcedID</a> banking trojan payload &#8211; code overlap with <a href="https://twitter.com/hashtag/pony?src=hash&amp;ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw">#pony</a> more details soon<a href="https://t.co/baS1fciJHX">https://t.co/baS1fciJHX</a><a href="https://twitter.com/malwrhunterteam?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw">@malwrhunterteam</a> <a href="https://twitter.com/campuscodi?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw">@campuscodi</a></p>
<p>&mdash; Jay Rosenberg (@jaytezer) <a href="https://twitter.com/jaytezer/status/930054725290078213?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw">November 13, 2017</a></p></blockquote>
<p><script data-minify="1" async src="https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com/wp-content/cache/min/1/widgets.js?ver=1741776118" charset="utf-8"></script></p>
<p>Pony is a trojan that was being distributed via the Hancitor downloader, distributed through Microsoft Word documents. The version of Pony used in the reports is believed to be the same threat actor as Vawtrak. It was also sold via underground forums until the source code was leaked online.</p>
<p><a href="https://analyze.intezer.com/#/analyses/00fd4dde-21b9-4661-8878-ee67b187362e" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><img fetchpriority="high" decoding="async" class="alignnone <p>Using the dive-in feature with the related Pony samples, we can see the following:</p>

<p style="text-align: center;">(Dive-in feature of <a href="https://intezer.com//intezer-analyze/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Intezer Analyze&#x2122;</a>)</p>
<p>With this information alone, it will be hard to attribute this sample to a certain threat actor due to the public availability of the source code of Pony.</p>
<p>Let’s take a look at some of the matching functions.</p>
<p><a href="https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com//wp-content/uploads/2017/11/GrabOutlook.png" data-slb-group="post-images" data-slb-active="1" data-slb-asset="2063569548" data-slb-internal="0" https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/11/GrabOutlook-1395x1530.png 1395w , https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/11/GrabOutlook-768x843.png 768w , https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/11/GrabOutlook-1400x1536.png 1400w , https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/11/GrabOutlook-595xh.png 595w " sizes="(max-width: 1637px) 100vw, 1637px" /></noscript></a></p>
<p>As we can see here, the function in these two samples is a 1:1 match. The function above is called GrabOutlook in the Pony source code and is responsible for stealing passwords from Outlook.  (You may notice a difference because the strings appear decrypted in the sample on the left as it looks like Proofpoint dumped the sample with the strings already decrypted before uploading to VirusTotal.)</p>
<p><a href="https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com//wp-content/uploads/2017/11/pony_2_0.png" data-slb-group="post-images" data-slb-active="1" data-slb-asset="890675064" data-slb-internal="0" <p style="text-align: center;">(GrabOutlook function from Pony 2.0 source code)</p>
<p>More specifically, we can tell the threat actor used code from version 2.0 of Pony because in the Pony 1.9 source code, we do not see calls to DecipherList which is responsible for decrypting the strings.</p>
<p><a href="https://149520725.v2.pressablecdn.com//wp-content/uploads/2017/11/pony_1_9.png" data-slb-group="post-images" data-slb-active="1" data-slb-asset="1463259614" data-slb-internal="0" <p style="text-align: center;">(GrabOutlook function from Pony 1.9 source code)</p>
<p>Other shared functions from Pony:</p>
<ul>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">OutlookExport</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">OutlookReadPSItemValue</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">OutlookScanPasswords</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">OutlookScanProfiles</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">PocomailScanReg</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">IncrediMailScanReg</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">CRC32Update</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">CommonCryptUnprotectData</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">MapFile</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">PonyStrCat</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">PonyStrCatFreeArg1</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">DecipherList</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">UnicodeToAnsiLen</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">FileExists</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">StreamUpdateModuleLen</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">StreamWriteModuleHeader</li>
</ul>
<p>There may be other functions from Pony, but we can see that the shared code is mostly related to stealing e-mail credentials.</p>
<p>Time and time again, we see threat actors reusing the same code. If we look at reused code, it makes it easier to detect malware. Such small code reuse makes it very difficult to get these kinds of conclusions by manually reverse engineering a file.  The ability to automate the finding of code reuse makes our job as malware analysts easier.</p>
<p>Report Samples:</p>
<ul>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">IcedID Dropper: 29f7469f8dc88820f72a9bdcb02badc1a40aa41b3f4b7f8caaa30409b3842aea</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">IcedID Payload: a6531184ea84bb5388d7c76557ff618d59f951c393a797950b2eb3e1d6307013</li>
<li style="font-weight: 400;">Pony: b19ec186f59b1f72c768ed2fcd8344d75821e527870b71e8123db96f683f1b68</li>
</ul>

<script type="rocketlazyloadscript">
jQuery(document).ready(function($) {
    var $slider = $('.testimonials-slider');
    var scrollSpeed = 1; // Lower values for slower scrolling
    var isDragging = false;
    var startX = 0, scrollStart = 0;
    var scrollInterval;
    
    if ($slider.length) {
        var $items = $slider.children();
        var scrollWidth = $slider[0].scrollWidth;
        var viewportWidth = $slider[0].clientWidth;

        $slider.append($items.clone()); // Clone items for smooth looping
        $slider.append($items.clone()); // Clone twice to ensure seamless loop

        function scrollLeft() {
            if (isDragging) return; // Stop scrolling while dragging

            var currentPosition = $slider.scrollLeft();
            var newPosition = currentPosition + scrollSpeed;

            // Instead of resetting instantly, adjust position dynamically
            if (newPosition >= scrollWidth) {
                newPosition = newPosition - scrollWidth; // Adjust position smoothly
            }

            $slider.scrollLeft(newPosition);
        }

        // Auto-scroll
        function startScrolling() {
            if (!scrollInterval) {
                scrollInterval = setInterval(scrollLeft, 20);
            }
        }

        function stopScrolling() {
            clearInterval(scrollInterval);
            scrollInterval = null; 
        }

        startScrolling(); // Start auto-scroll on load

        // Mouse & Touch Drag Support
        $slider.on('mousedown touchstart', function(e) {
            stopScrolling(); // Pause while interacting

            startX = e.type === 'touchstart' ? e.originalEvent.touches[0].pageX : e.pageX;
            scrollStart = $slider.scrollLeft();
            isDragging = true;
        });

        $slider.on('mousemove touchmove', function(e) {
            if (!isDragging) return;

            var x = e.type === 'touchmove' ? e.originalEvent.touches[0].pageX : e.pageX;
            var distance = startX - x;
            $slider.scrollLeft(scrollStart + distance);
        });

        $slider.on('mouseup touchend mouseleave', function() {
            isDragging = false;
            startScrolling(); // Resume auto-scroll after release
        });

        // Adjust on window resize
        $(window).on('resize', function() {
            viewportWidth = $slider[0].clientWidth; // Update viewport width dynamically
            scrollWidth = $slider[0].scrollWidth;
        });
    }
});


</script>

(function($) {
        $(document).ready(function() {

                fixCF7MultiSubmit();

                function fixCF7MultiSubmit(){
                                jQuery('input.wpcf7-submit[type="submit"]').click(function() {
                                        var disabled = jQuery(this).attr('data-disabled');
                                        if (disabled && disabled == "disabled") {
                                                return false;
                                 a       } else {
                                                jQuery(this).attr('data-disabled',"disabled");
                                                return true;
                                        }
                                });
                        jQuery('.wpcf7').bind("wpcf7submit",function(){
                                jQuery(this).find('input.wpcf7-submit[type="submit"]').attr('data-disabled',"enabled");
                        });
                }
        });
}(jQuery));
</script>
